perseus-eng2	You may plant a field well; but you know not who shall gather the fruits: you may build a house well; but you know not who shall dwell in it: able to command, you cannot know whether it is profitable to command: versed in statecraft, you know not whether it is profitable to guide the state: though, for your delight, you marry a pretty woman, you cannot tell whether she will bring you sorrow: though you form a party among men mighty in the state, you know not whether they will cause you to be driven from the state.

perseus-eng2	He did not even discuss that topic so favoured by other talkers, "the Nature of the Universe": and avoided speculation on the so-called "Cosmos" of the Professors, how it works, and on the laws that govern the phenomena of the heavens: indeed he would argue that to trouble one’s mind with such problems is sheer folly.

perseus-eng2	As some madmen have no fear of danger and others are afraid where there is nothing to be afraid of, as some will do or say anything in a crowd with no sense of shame, while others shrink even from going abroad among men, some respect neither temple nor altar nor any other sacred thing, others worship stocks and stones and beasts, so is it, he held, with those who worry with "Universal Nature."

perseus-eng2	Some hold that "What is" is one, others that it is infinite in number: some that all things are in perpetual motion, others that nothing can ever be moved at any time: some that all life is birth and decay, others that nothing can ever be born or ever die.

perseus-eng2	Nor were those the only questions he asked about such theorists. Students of human nature, he said, think that they will apply their knowledge in due course for the good of themselves and any others they choose.

cts:urn:tlg0032.tlg002:1.1.15

perseus-eng2	Do those who pry into heavenly phenomena imagine that, once they have discovered the laws by which these are produced, they will create at their will winds, waters, seasons and such things to their need? Or have they no such expectation, and are they satisfied with knowing the causes of these various phenomena?

perseus-eng2	The problems he discussed were, What is godly, what is ungodly; what is beautiful, what is ugly; what is just, what is unjust; what is prudence, what is madness; what is courage, what is cowardice; what is a state, what is a statesman; what is government, and what is a governor.

perseus-eng2	I remember that he once heard Criton say that life at Athens was difficult for a man who wanted to mind his own business.
perseus-eng2	"At this moment," Criton added, "actions are pending against me not because I have done the plaintiffs an injury, but because they think that I would sooner pay than have trouble."
perseus-eng2	"Tell me, Criton," said Socrates, "do you keep dogs to fend the wolves from your sheep?"
perseus-eng2	"Certainly," replied Criton,
perseus-eng2	"because it pays me better to keep them."
perseus-eng2	"Then why not keep a man who may be able and willing to fend off the attempts to injure you?"
perseus-eng2	"I would gladly do so were I not afraid that he might turn on me."
perseus-eng2	"What?"
perseus-eng2	"Don’t you see that it is much pleasanter to profit by humouring a man like you than by quarrelling with him?"
perseus-eng2	"I assure you there are men in this city who would take pride in your friendship.
perseus-eng2	Thereupon they sought out Archedemus, an excellent speaker and man of affairs, but poor.
perseus-eng2	For he was not one of those who make money unscrupulously, but an honest man, and he would say that it was easy to take forfeit from false accusers.
perseus-eng2	So whenever Criton was storing corn, oil, wine, wool, or other farm produce, he would make a present of a portion to Archedemus.
perseus-eng2	And when he sacrificed, he invited him, and in fact lost no similar opportunity of showing courtesy.
perseus-eng2	Archedemus came to regard Criton’s house as a haven of refuge and constantly paid his respects to him.
perseus-eng2	He soon found out that Criton’s false accusers had much to answer for and many enemies.
perseus-eng2	He brought one of them to trial on a charge involving damages or imprisonment.
perseus-eng2	The defendant, conscious that he was guilty on many counts, did all he could to get quit of Archedemus.
perseus-eng2	But Archedemus refused to let him off until he withdrew the action against Criton and compensated him.
perseus-eng2	Archedemus carried through several other enterprises of a similar kind; and now many of Criton’s friends begged him to make Archedemus their protector, just as when a shepherd has a good dog the other shepherds want to pen their flocks near his, in order to get the use of his dog.
perseus-eng2	Archedemus was glad to humour Criton, and so there was peace not only for Criton but for his friends as well.
perseus-eng2	If anyone whom he had offended reproached Archedemus with flattering Criton because he found him useful, he would answer, "Which, then, is disgraceful: to have honest men for your friends, by accepting and returning their favours, and to fall out with rogues; or to treat gentlemen as enemies by trying to injure them, and to make friends of rogues by siding with them, and to prefer their intimacy?"
perseus-eng2	Henceforward Archedemus was respected by Criton’s friends and was himself numbered among them.
perseus-eng2	Again I recall the following conversation between him and his companion Diodorus.
perseus-eng2	"Tell me, Diodorus," he said, "if one of your servants runs away, do you take steps to bring him back safe?"
perseus-eng2	"Certainly," he replied, "and I ask others to help in the search."
perseus-eng2	"Yes, of course," he replied, "and I invite others to help, by offering a reward for the recovery of the man."
perseus-eng2	"And further,"
perseus-eng2	"if one of your servants is ill, do you take care of him and call in doctors to prevent him dying?"
perseus-eng2	"Indeed I do."
perseus-eng2	"Well, suppose that one of your acquaintances, who is much more useful than your servants, is near being ruined by want, don’t you think it worth your while to take steps to save him?"
perseus-eng2	"Now you know that Hermogenes is a conscientious man and would be ashamed to take a favour from you without making a return."
perseus-eng2	"Yet surely it is worth many servants to have a willing, loyal, staunch subordinate, capable of doing what he is told, and not only so, but able to make himself useful unbidden, to think clearly and give advice."
perseus-eng2	"Good householders, you know, say that the right time to buy is when a valuable article can be bought at a low price;"
perseus-eng2	"and in these times the circumstances afford an opportunity of acquiring good friends very cheap."
perseus-eng2	"Thank you, Socrates," said Diodorus, "pray bid Hermogenes call on me."
perseus-eng2	"No, indeed I won’t," said he;
perseus-eng2	"for in my opinion it is at least as good for you to go to him yourself as to invite him to come to you, and you have quite as much to gain as he by doing so."
perseus-eng2	The consequence was that Diodorus set off to visit Hermogenes;
perseus-eng2	and in return for a small sum he acquired a friend who made a point of thinking how he could help and please him either by word or deed.
perseus-eng2	I will now explain how he helped those who were eager to win distinction by making them qualify themselves for the honours they coveted.
perseus-eng2	He once heard that Dionysodorus had arrived at Athens, and gave out that he was going to teach generalship. Being aware that one of his companions wished to obtain the office of general from the state, he addressed him thus:
perseus-eng2	"Young man, surely it would be disgraceful for one who wishes to be a general in the state to neglect the opportunity of learning the duties,"
perseus-eng2	"and he would deserve to be punished by the state much more than one who carved statues without having learned to be a sculptor."
perseus-eng2	"For in the dangerous times of war the whole state is in the general’s hands, and great good may come from his success and great evil from his failure."
perseus-eng2	"Therefore anyone who exerts himself to gain the votes, but neglects to learn the business, deserves punishment."
perseus-eng2	"This speech persuaded the man to go and learn."
perseus-eng2	"When he had learnt his lesson and returned, Socrates chaffed him."
perseus-eng2	"Don’t you think, sirs," he said, "that our friend looks more 'majestic,' as Homer called Agamemnon, now that he has learnt generalship?"
perseus-eng2	"For just as he who has learnt to play the harp is a harper even when he doesn’t play, and he who has studied medicine is a doctor even though he doesn’t practise, so our friend will be a general for ever, even if no one votes for him."
perseus-eng2	"But your ignoramus is neither general nor doctor, even if he gets every vote."
perseus-eng2	"But," he continued, "in order that any one of us who may happen to command a regiment or platoon under you may have a better knowledge of warfare, tell us the first lesson he gave you in generalship."
perseus-eng2	"The first was like the last," he replied; "he taught me tactics — nothing else."
perseus-eng2	"He taught me tactics and nothing else."
perseus-eng2	"But then that is only a small part of generalship," Socrates said.
perseus-eng2	"For a general must also be capable of furnishing military equipment and providing supplies for the men; he must be resourceful, active, careful, hardy and quick-witted; he must be both gentle and brutal, at once straightforward and designing, capable of both caution and surprise, lavish and rapacious, generous and mean, skilful in defence and attack; and there are many other qualifications, some natural, some acquired, that are necessary to one who would succeed as a general."
perseus-eng2	"It is well to understand tactics too;"
perseus-eng2	"for there is a wide difference between right and wrong disposition of the troops, just as stones, bricks, timber, and tiles flung together anyhow are useless, whereas when the materials that neither rot nor decay, that is, the stones and tiles, are placed at the bottom and the top, and the bricks and timber are put together in the middle, as in building, the result is something of great value, a house, in fact."
perseus-eng2	"Your analogy is perfect, Socrates," said the youth.
perseus-eng2	"For in war one must put the best men in the van and the rear, and the worst in the centre, that they may be led by the van and driven forward by the rearguard."
perseus-eng2	"Well and good, provided that he taught you also to distinguish the good and the bad men."
perseus-eng2	"If not, what have you gained by your lessons?"
perseus-eng2	"No more than you would have gained if he had ordered you to put the best money at the head and tail, and the worst in the middle, without telling you how to distinguish good from base coin."
perseus-eng2	"I assure you he didn’t,"
perseus-eng2	"so we should have to judge for ourselves which are the good men and which are the bad."
perseus-eng2	"Then we had better consider how we may avoid mistaking them."
perseus-eng2	"I want to do so," said the youth.
perseus-eng2	"Well now," said Socrates, "if we had to lay hands on a sum of money, would not the right arrangement be to put the most covetous men in the front?"
perseus-eng2	"I think so."
perseus-eng2	"And what should we do with those who are going to face danger?"
perseus-eng2	"Should our first line consist of the most ambitious?"
perseus-eng2	"Oh yes: they are the men who will face danger for the sake of glory."
perseus-eng2	"About these, now, there is no mystery: they are conspicuous everywhere, and so it is easy to find them."
perseus-eng2	"But," said Socrates, "did he teach you only the disposition of an army, or did he include where and how to use each formation?"
perseus-eng2	"Not at all," he replied.
perseus-eng2	"And yet there are many situations that call for a modification of tactics and strategy."
perseus-eng2	"I assure you he didn’t explain that."
perseus-eng2	"Then pray go back and ask him."
perseus-eng2	"If he knows and has a conscience, he will be ashamed to send you home ill-taught, after taking your money."
perseus-eng2	"One day when he met a man who had been chosen general, he asked him, 'For what reason, think you, is Agamemnon dubbed 'Shepherd of the people' by Homer?"
perseus-eng2	"Is it because a shepherd must see that his sheep are safe and are fed, and that the object for which they are kept is attained, and a general must see that his men are safe and are fed, and that the object for which they fight is attained, or, in other words, that victory over the enemy may add to their happiness?"
perseus-eng2	"They fight, indeed, to be more prosperous by overcoming the enemies."
perseus-eng2	"Or what reason can Homer have for praising Agamemnon by saying:"
perseus-eng2	"'both a good king and a doughty warrior too'?"
perseus-eng2	"Is it because he would be 'a doughty warrior too' not if he alone were a good fighter, but if he made all his men like himself; and 'a good king' not if he merely ordered his own life aright, but if he made his subjects happy as well?"
perseus-eng2	"Because a king is chosen, not to take good care of himself, but for the good of those who have chosen him;"
perseus-eng2	"and all men fight in order that they may get the best life possible, and choose generals to guide them to it."
perseus-eng2	"Therefore it is the duty of a commander to contrive this for those who have chosen him for general."
perseus-eng2	"For anything more honourable than that is not easy to find, or anything more disgraceful than its opposite."
perseus-eng2	"By these reflections on what constitutes a good leader he stripped away all other virtues, and left just the power to make his followers happy."
perseus-eng2	"Again, when someone had been chosen a leader of cavalry, I remember that Socrates conversed with him in the following manner:"
perseus-eng2	"Young man," he said, "can you tell us why you hankered after a cavalry command?"
perseus-eng2	"I presume it was not to be first of the cavalry in the charge;"
perseus-eng2	"for that privilege belongs to the mounted archers;"
perseus-eng2	"at any rate they ride ahead of their commanders even."
perseus-eng2	"True," he replied.

perseus-eng2	"Even madmen are known to everyone."

perseus-eng2	"But perhaps you think you can hand over the cavalry in better condition to the state when you retire, and can do something for the good of the state as a cavalry leader, in case there is any occasion to employ that arm?"
perseus-eng2	"Yes, certainly," he said.
perseus-eng2	"Yes," said Socrates, "and no doubt it is a fine thing if you can do that."
perseus-eng2	"The command, I presume, for which you have been chosen, is the command of horses and riders."
perseus-eng2	"Indeed it is."
perseus-eng2	"Come then, tell us first how you propose to improve the horses."
perseus-eng2	"Oh, but I don’t think that is my business. Every man must look after his own horse."
perseus-eng2	"Then if some of your men appear on parade with their horses ailing or suffering from bad feet or sore legs, others with underfed animals that can’t go the pace, others with restive brutes that won’t keep in line, others with such bad kickers that it is impossible to line them up at all, what will you be able to make of your cavalry?"
perseus-eng2	"how will you be able to do the state any good with a command like that?"
perseus-eng2	"I am much obliged to you," he replied, "and I will try to look after the horses carefully."
perseus-eng2	"Won’t you also try to improve the men?" asked Socrates.
perseus-eng2	"I will," he said.
perseus-eng2	"Then will you first train them to mount better?"
perseus-eng2	"Oh yes, I must," he replied, "so that if anyone is thrown he may have a better chance of saving himself."
perseus-eng2	"Further, when there is some danger before you, will you order them to draw the enemy into the sandy ground where your manoeuvres are held, or will you try to carry out your training in the kind of country that the enemy occupy?"
perseus-eng2	"Oh yes, that is the better way," he agreed.
perseus-eng2	"And again, will you pay much attention to bringing down as many of the enemy as possible without dismounting?"
perseus-eng2	"Oh yes, that too is the better way," he said.
perseus-eng2	"Have you thought of fostering a keen spirit among the men and hatred of the enemy, so as to make them more gallant in action?"

perseus-eng2	"Because without that horses and men, however good and gallant, are of no use."

perseus-eng2	 the best teachers rely most on the spoken word and those with the deepest knowledge of the greatest subjects are the best talkers?"
perseus-eng2	"Did you never reflect that, whenever one chorus is selected from the citizens of this state — for instance, the chorus that is sent to Delos — no choir from any other place can compare with it, and no state can collect so goodly a company?"
perseus-eng2	"You speak the truth," he said.
perseus-eng2	"And yet the reason is that Athenians excel all others not so much in singing or in stature or in strength, as in love of honour, which is the strongest incentive to deeds of honour and renown."

perseus-eng2	"I expect so," he said.
perseus-eng2	"Don’t hesitate then, but try to encourage this keenness among the men: both you and your fellow-citizens will benefit by the results of your efforts."
perseus-eng2	"Most certainly I will try," he said.

cts:urn:tlg0032.tlg002:4.8.11

perseus-eng2	2238	τῶν δὲ Σωκράτην γιγνωσκόντων , οἷος ἦν , οἱ ἀρετῆς ἐφιέμενοι πάντες ἔτι καὶ νῦν διατελοῦσι πάντων μάλιστα ποθοῦντες ἐκεῖνον , ὡς ὠφελιμώτατον ὄντα πρὸς ἀρετῆς ἐπιμέλειαν . This was the tenor of his conversation with Hermogenes and with the others. All who knew what manner of man Socrates was and who seek after virtue continue to this day to miss him beyond all others, as the chief of helpers in the quest of virtue.

cts:urn:tlg0032.tlg002:4.8.11
glaux1.0-sentence	2239
perseus-grc2	ἐμοὶ μὲν δή , τοιοῦτος ὢν οἷον ἐγὼ διήγημαι , εὐσεβὴς μὲν οὕτως ὥστε μηδὲν ἄνευ τῆς τῶν θεῶν γνώμης ποιεῖν , δίκαιος δὲ ὥστε βλάπτειν μὲν μηδὲ μικρὸν μηδένα , ὠφελεῖν δὲ τὰ μέγιστα τοὺς χρωμένους αὐτῷ , ἐγκρατὴς δὲ ὥστε μηδέποτε προαιρεῖσθαι τὸ ἥδιον ἀντὶ τοῦ βελτίονος , φρόνιμος δὲ ὥστε μὴ διαμαρτάνειν κρίνων τὰ βελτίω καὶ τὰ χείρω μηδὲ ἄλλου προσδεῖσθαι , ἀλλ’ αὐτάρκης εἶναι πρὸς τὴν τούτων γνῶσιν , ἱκανὸς δὲ καὶ λόγῳ εἰπεῖν τε καὶ διορίσασθαι τὰ τοιαῦτα , ἱκανὸς δὲ καὶ ἄλλως δοκιμάσαι τε καὶ ἁμαρτάνοντα ἐλέγξαι καὶ προτρέψασθαι ἐπ’ ἀρετὴν καὶ καλοκαγαθίαν , ἐδόκει τοιοῦτος εἶναι οἷος ἂν εἴη ἄριστός τε ἀνὴρ καὶ εὐδαιμονέστατος .
perseus-eng2	For myself, I have described him as he was: so religious that he did nothing without counsel from the gods; so just that he did no injury, however small, to any man, but conferred the greatest benefits on all who dealt with him; so self-controlled that he never chose the pleasanter rather than the better course; so wise that he was unerring in his judgment of the better and the worse, and needed no counsellor, but relied on himself for his knowledge of them; masterly in expounding and defining such things; no less masterly in putting others to the test, and convincing them of error and exhorting them to follow virtue and gentleness. To me then he seemed to be all that a truly good and happy man must be.

cts:urn:tlg0032.tlg002:4.8.11

perseus-grc2	εἰ δέ τῳ μὴ ἀρέσκει ταῦτα , παραβάλλων τὸ ἄλλων ἦθος πρὸς ταῦτα οὕτω κρινέτω .
perseus-eng2	But if there is any doubter, let him set the character of other men beside these things; then let him judge.
